        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966"><meta property="og:title" content="MoS2, WS2, and MoWS2 Flakes as Reversible Host Materials for Sodium-Ion and Potassium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/acsomega.4c01966.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Transition-metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) and their alloys are vital for the development of sustainable and economical energy storage alternatives due to their large interlayer spacing and hosting ability for alkali-metal ions. Although the Li-ion chemically correlates with the Na-ion and K-ion, research on batteries with TMD anodes for K+ is still in its infancy. This research explores TMDs such as molybdenum disulfide (MoS2) and tungsten disulfide (WS2) and TMD alloys such as molybdenum tungsten disulfide (MoWS2) for both sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (KIBs). The cyclic stability test analysis indicates that in the initial cycle, the MoS2 NIB demonstrates exceptional performance, with a peak charge capacity of 1056 mAh g–1, while retaining high Coulombic efficiency. However, the WS2 KIB underperforms, with the least charge capacity of 130 mAh g–1 in the first cycle and exceptionally low retention at a current density of 100 mA g–1. The MoWS2 TMD alloy exhibits a moderate charge capacity and cyclic efficiency for both NIBs and KIBs. This comparison study shows that decreasing  sizes of alkali-metal ions and constituent elements in TMDs or TMD alloys leads to decreased resistance and slower degradation processes as indicated by cyclic voltammetry and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy after 10 cycles. Furthermore, the study of probable electrochemical intercalation and removal processes of Na-ions and K-ions demonstrates that large geometrically shaped TMD flakes are more responsive to intercalation for Na-ions than K-ions. These performance comparisons of different TMD materials for NIBs and KIBs may promote the future development of these batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/acsomega.4c01966.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318a78f49e0222a-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318a78f49e0222a-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '72fe850b-0ef4-415f-95af-080fc224544a'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

charset="utf-8" src="https://pubs.acs.org/products/achs/releasedAssets/js/requestidlecallback-polyfill-f5170b9de43ccc25622c.js"></script><script id="yui__dyn_0" type="text/javascript" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318a78f49e0222a-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'omega';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/omega/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/omega/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="9318a78f49e0222a-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "acsodf";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MoS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, and MoWS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Flakes as Reversible Host Materials for Sodium-Ion and Potassium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c01966", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MoS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, and MoWS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Flakes as Reversible Host Materials for Sodium-Ion and Potassium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c01966", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MoS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, and MoWS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Flakes as Reversible Host Materials for Sodium-Ion and Potassium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c01966", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MoS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, and MoWS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Flakes as Reversible Host Materials for Sodium-Ion and Potassium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c01966", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MoS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, and MoWS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Flakes as Reversible Host Materials for Sodium-Ion and Potassium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c01966", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MoS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, and MoWS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Flakes as Reversible Host Materials for Sodium-Ion and Potassium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c01966", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MoS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, and MoWS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Flakes as Reversible Host Materials for Sodium-Ion and Potassium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c01966", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MoS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, and MoWS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Flakes as Reversible Host Materials for Sodium-Ion and Potassium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c01966", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MoS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;, and MoWS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Flakes as Reversible Host Materials for Sodium-Ion and Potassium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c01966", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

                                            </p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --><div class="articleMetrics articleMetrics--article"><div class="articleMetrics_table"><div class="articleMetrics_row"><input type="hidden" name="articleMetricsDoi" value="10.1021/acsomega.4c01966"><div class="articleMetrics_metric"><h2>Article Views</h2><div tabindex="-1" aria-describedby="views-tooltip" class="articleMetrics-val articleMetrics-tooltip"><span>1355</span></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_altmetric"><h2>Altmetric</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val articleMetrics_score">-</div><div class="altmetric-donut hidden"><div data-badge-details="right" data-badge-type="donut" data-hide-no-mentions="false" data-condensed="true" class="altmetric-embed"></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">3</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0013.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0013.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Transition-metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) and their alloys are vital for the development of sustainable and economical energy storage alternatives due to their large interlayer spacing and hosting ability for alkali-metal ions. Although the Li-ion chemically correlates with the Na-ion and K-ion, research on batteries with TMD anodes for K<sup>+</sup> is still in its infancy. This research explores TMDs such as molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>) and tungsten disulfide (WS<sub>2</sub>) and TMD alloys such as molybdenum tungsten disulfide (MoWS<sub>2</sub>) for both sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (KIBs). The cyclic stability test analysis indicates that in the initial cycle, the MoS<sub>2</sub> NIB demonstrates exceptional performance, with a peak charge capacity of 1056 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, while retaining high Coulombic efficiency. However, the WS<sub>2</sub> KIB underperforms, with the least charge capacity of 130 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the first cycle and exceptionally low retention at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The MoWS<sub>2</sub> TMD alloy exhibits a moderate charge capacity and cyclic efficiency for both NIBs and KIBs. This comparison study shows that decreasing  sizes of alkali-metal ions and constituent elements in TMDs or TMD alloys leads to decreased resistance and slower degradation processes as indicated by cyclic voltammetry and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy after 10 cycles. Furthermore, the study of probable electrochemical intercalation and removal processes of Na-ions and K-ions demonstrates that large geometrically shaped TMD flakes are more responsive to intercalation for Na-ions than K-ions. These performance comparisons of different TMD materials for NIBs and KIBs may promote the future development of these batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under</p><div class="article__cc-license__inter"><div class="article__cc-license--hoverable"><a href="https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/4.0/" aria-describedby="tooltip-cc-text-abstract" class="link--bold" data-id="article_abstract_CC-License">CC-BY-NC-ND 4.0
                                    <span class="arrow"></span></a>
                                .

                                                actual license. It is not a license and has no legal value. Carefully
                                                review the actual license before using these materials.
                                            </p></div></div></div></div><ul class="article__cc-license__list"><li class="article__cc-license__item"><a aria-describedby="tooltip-cc-0" class="article__cc-license__link" data-id="article_abstract_CC-License_IconCC"><span class="arrow"></span><img src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/cc-license/cc.svg" alt="cc licence" width="22" height="22" class="article__cc-license__icon"></a><div role="tooltip" id="tooltip-cc-0" class="article__hover" data-id="article_abstract_CC-IconTooltip"><div class="article__tooltip"><h3>License Summary*</h3><p>You are free to <span class="bold">share</span>(copy and redistribute)

Published by American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291021&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Alloys"> Alloys </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells"> Electrochemical cells </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292275&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transmission electron microscopy"> Transmission electron microscopy </a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The invention and subsequent commercial success of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have powered modern consumer electronics and the transportation industry due to the use of low-cost graphite as an anode material for LIBs; graphite can cycle Li-ions for hundreds of cycles with nearly 100% efficiency. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> However, the pursuit of sustainable electrochemical energy storage systems for renewable energy integration, as well as increasing global supply chain constraints regarding LIB constituents (e.g., lithium, cobalt, and nickel), requires batteries that are built using renewable sources and environmentally friendly materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> Consequently, increased research has focused on other alkali-metal-ion-based batteries, such as sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (KIBs), because sodium and potassium have more uniform, abundant distribution in the Earth crust compared to a mere 0.0017 wt % <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a> of lithium. Recent studies have suggested several potential cathode materials and low-cost electrolyte salts for NIBs and KIBs that are analogous to those in use for commercial LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5−7)</a> However, a suitable anode has yet to be found because Na/graphite and K/graphite compounds may not be thermodynamically stable. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 8 to 11">(8−11)</a> Other studies have concluded that the larger radius of the Na-ion (1.02 Å) and K-ion (1.38 Å) compared to that of the Li-ion (0.76 Å) could be the reason for low capacity and poor cycling stability for NIBs and KIBs when graphite is used as the anode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 10 to 13">(10−13)</a> Although recent findings involving other forms of carbon, such as carbon black, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> microcrystalline structured carbon, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> and cellulose-derived carbon <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> as electrodes or protective layers for electrodes and those involving the use of emerging polymer <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a> or ionic liquid electrolytes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 19 to 22">(19−22)</a> have yielded promising results in NIBs and KIBs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 23 to 25">(23−25)</a> the search for a commercially viable electrode architecture is far from over.</div><div class="NLM_p">This search for an ideal anode for NIBs and KIBs has led to increased interest in other layered lamellar structures, including transition-metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) that have similar morphology to graphite but increased interlayer spacings. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Approximately 40 different TMDs have been discovered so far, of which group VI is predominantly layered; examples include molybdenum (MoS<sub>2</sub>) and tungsten dichalcogenides (WS<sub>2</sub>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 to 29">(27−29)</a> MoS<sub>2</sub> has been investigated extensively for LIBs and NIBs <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> along with the development of modified nanostructures such as nanotubes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> nanosheets, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> nanoflowers, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> nanoboxes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> nanospheres, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> etc. Also, the few studies that have utilized WS<sub>2</sub> for NIBs used various modified micro/nanostructures of WS<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> including hollow microspheres, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> nanoplates, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> and WS<sub>2</sub>-3DrGO. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> However, by alloying and doping different transition metals or chalcogen atoms, TMD alloys (i.e., Mo<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>W<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub>2</sub>) have been shown to bring flexibility to the crystal structure and physical properties, which may unveil tunable band gaps as a function of layers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> For example, the tuning range of the band gap is 1.8–2.0 eV for MoWS<sub>2</sub> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> compared to MoS<sub>2</sub> with a band gap of 1.9 eV <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> and WS<sub>2</sub> with a band gap of 2.1 eV. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Tunability of such a band gap allows better charge transfer and higher conductivity, leading to improved specific capacitance and energy density. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> In another recent study, an enlarged interlayer spacing of Mo<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>W<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub>2</sub> nanosheets indicated superior hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) performance, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> which suggests that doping TMDs with other transition-metal ions improves the efficacy of TMD alloys as catalysts. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The range of band gaps and improved HER performance indicate superior prospects for TMDs and TMD alloys in future energy storage development.</div><div class="NLM_p">Although TMDs and TMD alloys have shown promising electrode material properties, structural aggregation, electrochemical instability, low reversibility, and rapid capacity decline have always been a challenge. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> This work investigated and compared the electrochemical performance of WS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets as working electrodes in half-cells of KIBs and NIBs. The interpretation of internal structural distortion and performance consistency has also been focused on in this work to compare the commonly studied TMDs (WS<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>) with the rarely investigated MoWS<sub>2</sub> TMD alloy as an electrode for NIBs and KIBs. This study employed cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) to comprehend faradaic and non-faradaic contributions, the effect of scan rate, and cell degradation processes. A comparison with a previous study on MoWSe<sub>2</sub> electrodes <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> revealed significant insight into the role of the atomic size of the constituent elements in the TMDs and TMD alloys.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Analysis of the cyclic stability test revealed that the MoS<sub>2</sub> NIB has the highest charge capacity of 1056 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the first cycle, with the highest retention capacity of 71%, while the WS<sub>2</sub> KIB has the lowest charge capacity of 130 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the first cycle and only 29% retention capacity at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The MoWS<sub>2</sub> TMD alloy showed intermediate charge capacity and cyclic efficiency for both NIBs and KIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Materials, Methods, and Characterization</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Morphologic and Spectroscopic Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In this study, all of the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images were obtained using a Hitachi SU8010 microscope, and the X-ray fluorescence (XRF) spectrum was collected using the IXRF system on a Hitachi SEM, which is equipped with an X-ray fluorescence and detection capability. Also, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) of the bulk MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub> was carried out using an FEI Tecnai Osiris 200 kV, and the Philips CM100 microscope was used for selected area electron diffraction (SAED) images. A Renishaw inVia Raman microscope was used to collect Raman spectra with a 532 nm laser. The Thermo Fisher scientific instrument was used to obtain X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) data, equipped with a monochromatic Al anode Kα (<i>h</i>ν = 1486.6 eV) with in situ sputtering of Ar<sup>+</sup> at 3.0 keV for 120 s. The PANalytical Empyrean was used to collect X-ray diffraction (XRD) data at 45 kV, a 40 mA power setting, and a step size of 0.02°.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Electrochemical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All of the electrodes in this study (MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub>) were prepared using a similar wt % ratio. 70 wt % of MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, or MoWS<sub>2</sub> powder as received (Ossila, Sheffield, U.K.) were mixed with 15 wt % of carbon black (Alfa Aesar, MA) as conducting agent and 15 wt % of polyvinylidene fluoride (Alfa Aesar, MA) as binder. All of the mass measurements were conducted using an analytical balance with a readability of 0.1 mg and a repeatability of 0.08 mg. A few drops of <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone (Sigma-Aldrich, MO) were also mixed as a solvent to obtain a homogeneous slurry, which was coated onto a Cu foil (thickness = 9 μm). After the samples were dried overnight at 80 °C, circular electrodes with a diameter of 14.29 mm were punched out from the coated Cu foils. These circular electrodes at an active mass loading of approximately 0.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> were used as working electrodes, while the counter electrodes were made of Na and K metals for Na-ion and K-ion half-cells, respectively. One M NaClO<sub>4</sub> (Alfa Aesar) in (1:1 v/v) ethylene carbonate (EC)/dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (anhydrous, 99% Sigma-Aldrich) and 1.0 M KPF<sub>6</sub> (Alfa Aesar) in (1:1 v/v) EC/DMC were used as electrolytes for NIBs and KIBs, respectively. At high precision in an inert Ar atmosphere (inside the glovebox), the coin cells (CR-2032) were assembled, where the O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O contents were kept below 0.1 ppm throughout the process. The electrochemical behavior of the assembled cells was investigated using the Arbin BT 2000 test unit (four channels in current ranges: 0.1, 0.001, and 0.00001 A with a maximum channel power of 1 W). The cyclic stability test was carried out with a current density of 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The electrochemical workstation from CH Instruments (model CHI660E) was used to carry out the cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). Based on the manufacturer’s specifications, the electrochemical workstation has applied potential and current measurement accuracy of ±1 mV (±0.01% of scale) and 0.2%, respectively. The measured current resolution is 0.0015% of the current range (minimum of 0.3 fA). Then, the cycled half-cells were decrimped in a similar Ar atmosphere, and the recovered electrodes were dried at 80 °C for 24 h.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The lateral size, morphology, and crystallinity of MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub> powders were studied by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a–c shows the TEM image for MoS<sub>2</sub>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d–f shows WS<sub>2</sub>, and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g–k shows MoWS<sub>2</sub>. The thin sheet-like morphology and rough edges of thin MoS<sub>2</sub> flakes are evident in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a,b; these were likely the result of the TEM sample preparation process (i.e., liquid exfoliated flakes suspended on a lacey carbon grid). The corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern of the MoS<sub>2</sub> sample (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c) revealed a six-spot hexagonal pattern with (101), (008), and (200) planes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> indicating the crystallinity. For the WS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, the layered structure with a 120° edge angle was apparent, which is typical of TMD materials, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d,e. The corresponding SAED pattern in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f showed (002), (100), and (101) planes, which validate a well-crystalline structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The high-resolution TEM images taken along the edges of the MoWS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets showed densely packed stripe-like overlapping grains (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>h derived from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g is analyzed via fast Fourier transformation (FFT), inverse FFT based on bright spots, and fringe intensity profile to investigate the interfringe distance. These analyses revealed the crystal symmetry of the MoWS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with a lattice constant of approximately 0.27 nm, which was in agreement with the interlayer lattice spacing value of the (100) plane from MoS<sub>2</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub>. The low-resolution TEM image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>i,j demonstrates the existence of triangular domains, which is typical of layered group VI TMD materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The subsequent SAED pattern of such a domain (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>k) exhibited a six-spot hexagonal pattern with a combination of MoS<sub>2</sub>-like (002), (101), and (107) and WS<sub>2</sub>-like (004), (103), and (1110) planes. Overall, the TMD flakes were approximately 1–2 μm in size, which matched the manufacturer’s specifications.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Low-resolution TEM images of (a, b) MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and (c) corresponding SAED pattern; low-resolution TEM images of (d, e) WS<sub>2</sub> powders and (f) corresponding SAED pattern; high-resolution TEM images of (g, h) MoWS<sub>2</sub> powders depicting fringe distances; low-resolution TEM images of (i, j) MoWS<sub>2</sub> powders and (k) corresponding SAED pattern.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a shows the Raman spectra of the as-obtained MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub> powders. The Raman spectrum of MoS<sub>2</sub> demonstrates prominent E<sub>2g</sub><sup>1</sup> and A<sub>1g</sub> modes at 378.55 and 404.05 cm<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> For the neat WS<sub>2</sub>, the high-intensity E<sub>2g</sub><sup>1</sup> and A<sub>1g</sub> modes were at 354.38 and 418.18 cm<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> While the in-plane E<sub>2g</sub><sup>1</sup> vibration mode denoted the optical vibration of transition metals and chalcogen atoms, the out-of-plane A<sub>1g</sub> vibration denoted the optical vibration of chalcogen atoms. These observations are consistent with the literature for high-purity bulk crystals. For the MoWS<sub>2</sub> alloy sample, the mode at 413.95 cm<sup>–1</sup> was assigned to A<sub>1g</sub> because it was between the A<sub>1g</sub> modes of MoS<sub>2</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub>. The other two prominent peaks at 351.54 and 379.97 cm<sup>–1</sup> were assigned to WS<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>-like E<sub>2g</sub><sup>1</sup> modes, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> This observation differs from previous works involving Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>W<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>S<sub>2</sub> ultrathin sheets, which reported two Raman bands for bulk MoWS<sub>2</sub> instead of the three bands reported in this study. Nonetheless, shifting of the A<sub>1g</sub> mode of MoS<sub>2</sub> to a higher wavenumber due to W doping in the MoS<sub>2</sub> E<sub>2g</sub><sup>1</sup> mode was noted. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> This two-mode behavior of MoWS<sub>2</sub> can be correlated with a 1.92 times larger atomic weight of W compared to Mo since this mass difference causes both metal atoms to vibrate. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Raman spectra of MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub> powders and (b) XRD reflection of MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub> electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">X-ray diffraction (XRD) was utilized on materials coated on Cu foils to identify the crystallinity of the MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub> electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b). For the MoS<sub>2</sub> sample, peaks at 14.49, 29.12, 43.40, 50.54, 60.22, and 74.18° were ascribed to the (002), (004), (006), (105), (008), and (116) crystal planes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> respectively (JCPDS card no. 00-037-1492). However, reflections from WS<sub>2</sub> suggested peaks at 14.14, 29.35, 43.79, 50.92, 60.30, and 74.55°, indicating the existence of (002), (004), (006), (105), (112), and (116) crystal planes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> respectively (JCPDS card no. 00-008-0237). The MoWS<sub>2</sub> electrode demonstrated a mix of reflections from MoS<sub>2</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub>; specifically, (002), (004), (006), (105), and (116) crystal planes occurred in the diffractogram with <i>d</i>-spacings of 6.14, 3.07, 2.09, 1.81, and 1.28 Å, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) of the electrodes showed surface characteristics with the chemical and electronic states of the material. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a shows the XPS survey scan of MoWS<sub>2</sub>, while <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b–d shows the high-resolution scans of the Mo 3d, W 4f, and S 2p spectra, respectively. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b, the Mo 3d high-resolution spectral deconvolution reveals one peak of Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 232.25 eV and another peak of Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub> at 228.97 eV, both attributed to MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> Additionally, at higher binding energy for oxidized MoS<sub>2</sub>, the ion contributions were nearly identical, such as MoO<sub>2</sub> and MoO<sub>3</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> In the fitted high-resolution W 4f spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c), two peaks at 33.28 and 35.48 eV were identified for W 4f<sub>7/2</sub> and W 4f<sub>5/2</sub>, respectively, which were ascribed to the +4 valence state of tungsten. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a> Another peak at 38.97 eV indicated the presence of WO<sub><i>x</i></sub> due to partial oxidization. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a> The S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks at 161.88 and 162.98 eV binding energies, respectively, defined the divalent states of metal sulfide. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XPS survey scan representing the presence of different elements within MoWS<sub>2</sub>; high-resolution (b) Mo 3d, (c) W 4f, and (d) S 2p XPS spectra of the MoWS<sub>2</sub> material demonstrating the chemical bonding states.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) and differential or incremental capacity curves derived from GCD curves revealed reactions at various stages during charging and discharging cycles. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a1 shows GCD plots for the MoS<sub>2</sub> NIB half-cell for the first two cycles with the first-cycle differential capacity curve (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a2), showing specific peaks at 0.92, 0.8, 0.47, and 0.12 V during sodiation. Although the 0.92 and 0.8 V peaks could be ascribed to the insertion of Na-ions into MoS<sub>2</sub> layers, the 0.47 V peak could be due to the conversion reaction between Na-ions and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub>. The remaining 0.1 V peak may be the result of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer on MoS<sub>2</sub> during initial sodiation since the intensity of forming the SEI layer decreased in the consecutive sodiation cycle. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> In the second cycle, the 0.92 V peak showed slight polarization, moving to 1.02 V, and in the first desodiation process, peaks were visible at 0.08, 1.25, 1.72, and 2.16 V, potentially due to reverse insertion and conversion reactions during desodiation.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a1) GCD and (a2) differential capacity curves of MoS<sub>2</sub> NIB electrodes; (a3) GCD and (a4) differential capacity curves of MoS<sub>2</sub> KIB electrodes; (b1) GCD and (b2) differential capacity curves of WS<sub>2</sub> NIB electrodes; (b3) GCD and (b4) differential capacity curves of WS<sub>2</sub> KIB electrodes; (c1) GCD and (c2) differential capacity curves of MoWS<sub>2</sub> NIB electrodes, and (c3) GCD and (c4) differential capacity curves of MoWS<sub>2</sub> KIB electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The GCD curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a3) and derived differential capacity curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a4) highlight the potassiation/depotassiation reactions at different voltages. In the first potassiation process, the peaks at 1.10 and 0.44 V may be attributed to K-ions intercalating into MoS<sub>2</sub> layers and conversion reactions to generate Mo and K<sub>2</sub>S. These peaks lost their intensity in the second cycle onward. Other peaks in the 0.01–0.3 V range could be due to irreversible SEI formation. Reversible intercalation and conversion reaction peaks were visible in the depotassiation process at 0.67 and 1.58 V, similarly losing intensity from the second cycle onward. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a></div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b1 depicts the GCD curves from which the differential capacity curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b2) of the WS<sub>2</sub> NIB half-cells were derived. The first-cycle sodiation curve showed peaks at 0.61, 0.43, and 0.30 V. Although the peak at 0.61 V may be attributed to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into WS<sub>2</sub> to form Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>WS<sub>2</sub>, the other two peaks at 0.43 and 0.30 V could be ascribed to the conversion reaction and SEI layer formation. In the first-cycle desodiation process, peaks at 1.82 and 2.28 V could be attributed to sodium extraction and back conversion reaction, forming W to WS<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> In the second cycle, the insertion and conversion reactions moved to 1.96 and 1.33 V during sodiation, respectively, but in the second cycle, desodiation process, Na<sup>+</sup> extraction, and reverse conversion peaks demonstrated slight polarization behavior, moving to 1.83 and 2.3 V, respectively, with much lower intensity.</div><div class="NLM_p">First-cycle potassiation and depotassiation GCD curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b3) and derived differential capacity curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b4) depicted the K<sup>+</sup> interaction with the WS<sub>2</sub>. In the first-cycle potassiation, peaks appeared at 1.33, 0.93, 0.76, 0.67, 0.51, and 0.37 V. Peaks on or above 0.76 V may be attributed to the insertion of K-ions into the WS<sub>2</sub> lattice to form K<sub><i>x</i></sub>WS<sub>2</sub>, while the peaks below 0.76 V may be ascribed to the conversion reaction to form K<sub><i>x</i></sub>S and metallic W and the construction of the SEI film. In the depotassiation process, peaks formed at 1.33, 0.70, and 0.31 V, with the broad peak at 1.33 V potentially signifying the extraction of K-ions from WS<sub>2</sub> and the other peaks signifying the reverse conversion reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref66" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref66" aria-label="reference 66">(66)</a> In the second-cycle potassiation process, the insertion and conversion reaction peaks moved to 0.85 and 0.50 V, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">For the MoWS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, the GCD curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c1) and derived differential capacity curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c2) illustrated multistep reactions during the sodiation/desodiation process. During the first sodiation cycle, the peaks at 1.5 and 0.05 V may correlate with the alloy insertion and conversion reactions within MoS<sub>2</sub> lattices. Because the peaks at 0.78, 0.53, and 0.19 V were absent in the subsequent cycle, they were ascribed to irreversible SEI layer formation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref67" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref67" aria-label="reference 67">(67)</a> In the first desodiation process, broad peaks at 2.26 and 1.82 V were attributed to Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and reverse conversion reaction from both WS<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub> lattices within the MoWS<sub>2</sub> alloy, while in the second cycle, a pair of peaks appeared at 1.39/1.83 and 2.07/2.26 V during the sodiation/desodiation processes, similar to neat MoS<sub>2</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub> electrodes.</div><div class="NLM_p">The first cycle of potassiation/depotassiation GCD (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c3) and derived differential capacity curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c4) contained multiple peaks that resembled both MoS<sub>2</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub> electrodes. For example, the first-cycle potassiation step exhibited WS<sub>2</sub>-like reactions at 0.9–1.0 and 0.73 V and MoS<sub>2</sub>-like reactions at 0.44 V. The first depotassiation step resulted in broad peaks at the 1.9–2.2 and 1.17–1.30 V regions, potentially due to the K-ion interaction with both MoS<sub>2</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub> lattices within the MoWS<sub>2</sub> alloy. The small and broad peak at 0.69 V was only attributed to K<sup>+</sup> reacting with WS<sub>2</sub>. Compared to the first cycle, the second-cycle potassiation/depotassiation process demonstrated less intense peaks at the 0.71/1.38 and 1.04/1.61 V regions.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a> shows the long-term stability of all three neat electrodes in Na- and K-ion half-cell systems. During Na<sup>+</sup> storage, in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, the MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode demonstrated the highest specific charge capacity of 1056 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, followed by MoWS<sub>2</sub> (442 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and WS<sub>2</sub> (293 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). A similar trend was also observed for the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency (MoS<sub>2</sub>: 71% &gt; MoWS<sub>2</sub>: 70% &gt; WS<sub>2</sub>: 52%). The low first-cycle Coulombic efficiency correlated well with the irreversible reactions observed during the sodiation step or SEI layer formation. All three cells showed gradual capacity fading behavior beginning in the second cycle, but, after repeated rigorous sodiation/desodiation cycles for 100 cycles, the MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode demonstrated the highest specific charge capacity (68 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), followed by MoWS<sub>2</sub> (32 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and WS<sub>2</sub> (25 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). The capacity values are lower compared to other research involving electrodes from high-surface-area TMD nanosheets or conducting TMD nanohybrids with carbon, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref68 ref69 ref70" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref68 ref69 ref70" aria-label="reference 49 and 68 to 70">(49,68−70)</a> which are typically synthesized via bottom-up or wet-chemical synthesis. This expected discrepancy in the values is present because bulk or as-received TMD materials were used for the present work without nanostructuring. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref71" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref71" aria-label="reference 29 and 71">(29,71)</a> The performance comparisons with different synthesis processes and this work are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/suppl_file/ao4c01966_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Cycling stability test and corresponding Coulombic efficiency of MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub> electrodes in (a) Na-ion half-cells and (b) K-ion half-cells, along with rate constant (<i>k</i>) and half-life values (<i>t</i><sub>1/2</sub>) determined by exponential decay fitting.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Compared with Na-ion storage, all three materials demonstrated lower K-ion storage (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b). However, much like NIBs, MoS<sub>2</sub> possessed the highest specific charge capacity (549 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), followed by MoWS<sub>2</sub> (472 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and WS<sub>2</sub> (130 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). The first-cycle Coulombic efficiency for all three electrodes followed a similar trend (MoS<sub>2</sub>: 46% &gt; MoWS<sub>2</sub>: 43% &gt; WS<sub>2</sub>: 29%), but the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency of the KIB cells was much lower than the NIBs. The specific charge capacities of all three cells gradually faded from the second cycle.
