After continuous cycling for 100 potassiation/depotassiation steps, MoS<sub>2</sub> had the highest capacity (96 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), followed by MoWS<sub>2</sub> (36 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and WS<sub>2</sub> (22 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>).</div><div class="NLM_p">The specific charge capacity plot was fitted with the following equation of the exponential decay function<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-1"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-2"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-3"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-4"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-5">y</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-6"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-7" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-8"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-9"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-10" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">y</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-11" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">0</span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-12" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-13"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-14" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">A</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-15" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">1</span></span><span class="MJXp-mspace" id="MJXp-Span-16" style="width: 0.25em; height: 0em;"></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-17"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-18" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">e</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-19" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-20">−</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-21">x</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-22">/</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-23"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-24" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">t</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-25" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">1</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-1-Frame" tabindex="0" style=""></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>y</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>y</mi><mn>0</mn></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>A</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><mspace width="0.25em"></mspace><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi><mrow><mo>−</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>/</mo><msub><mi>t</mi><mn>1</mn></msub></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div>where <i>y</i><sub>0</sub> is offset, <i>A</i><sub>1</sub> is amplitude, and <i>t</i><sub>1</sub> is time constant. Parameters such as half-life (<i>t</i><sub>1/2</sub>) and decay constant (<i>k</i>) were also evaluated by using the nonlinear fit. The half-life refers to the duration it takes for half of the initial charge capacity to decay or transform. The shorter the half-life, the faster the exponential decay occurs. The <i>t</i><sub>1/2</sub> of the performance of a half-cell can be determined using the following equation <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref72 ref73" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref72 ref73" aria-label="reference 72 and 73">(72,73)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-26"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-27"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-28"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-29"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-30"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-31" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">t</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-32" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-33">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-34">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-35">2</span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-36"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-37" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-38"><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-39" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-40"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-41">ln</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-42" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.25em;"></span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-43">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-44">k</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-2-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>t</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mi>ln</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace="0.25em">⁡</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow><mi>k</mi></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div>Here, <i>k</i> measures the rate of decay, which is constant throughout the decay process for a given half-cell. In Na-ion storage (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a), the MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode exhibited the lowest decay constant (<i>k</i> = MoS<sub>2</sub>: 0.10 &lt; MoWS<sub>2</sub>: 0.16 &lt; WS<sub>2</sub>: 0.19) and highest half-life (<i>t</i><sub>1/2</sub> = MoS<sub>2</sub>: 6.87 &gt; MoWS<sub>2</sub>: 4.33 &gt; WS<sub>2</sub>: 3.60). However, K-ion storage showed a similar result for the MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b), including the lowest decay constant (<i>k</i> = MoS<sub>2</sub>: 0.03 &lt; WS<sub>2</sub>: 0.05 &lt; MoWS<sub>2</sub>: 0.06) and highest half-life (<i>t</i><sub>1/2</sub> = MoS<sub>2</sub>: 21.44 &gt; WS<sub>2</sub>: 14.67 &gt; MoWS<sub>2</sub>: 12.27) and MoWS<sub>2</sub> exhibited the lowest half-life among the KIB half-cells.</div><div class="NLM_p">A comparison of these data with a previous study of MoWSe<sub>2</sub> TMD <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>) showed that, for both Na-ion and K-ion storage, the presence of molybdenum and its ratio in the TMD alloy most significantly affected charge capacity and capacity retention. A larger atomic (covalent) radius of tungsten (1.50 Å) compared to that of molybdenum (1.46 Å) was shown to potentially limit the intercalation of sodium and potassium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref74 ref75" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref74 ref75" aria-label="reference 74 and 75">(74,75)</a> Therefore, WS<sub>2</sub> exhibited the lowest charge capacity (NIB: 293 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and KIB: 130 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and lowest capacity retention (NIB: 52% and KIB: 29%). However, the inclusion of molybdenum increased the intercalation capacity, which led to higher charge capacity (NIB: 442 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and KIB: 472 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and higher cyclic efficiency (NIB: 70% and KIB: 43%) for MoWS<sub>2</sub> electrodes. Also, selenium (covalent radius of 1.18 Å) instead of sulfur (covalent radius of 1.04 Å) mitigated the reaction between TMDs and alkali ions due to its larger size. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref74" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref74" aria-label="reference 74">(74)</a> Consequently, the MoWSe<sub>2</sub> NIB demonstrated a 353 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> charge capacity, while the MoWSe<sub>2</sub> KIB half-cell exhibited only 291 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Both MoWS<sub>2</sub> and MoWSe<sub>2</sub> TMD alloys revealed very similar capacity retention for the first cycle, but only molybdenum and sulfur (MoS<sub>2</sub>) electrodes provided the highest charge capacity (NIB: 1056 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and KIB: 549 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and superior capacity retention (NIB: 71% and KIB: 46%).</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. First-cycle charge capacity and Coulombic efficiency comparison between all of the half-cells of this study and the MoWSe<sub>2</sub> NIB and KIB from another study reported in ref  <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a></p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">This study conducted cyclic voltammetry (CV) experiments at different scan rates to determine the fast physical charge storage mechanism within the anode material of MoWS<sub>2</sub> and further compared it to the base material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref76" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref76" aria-label="reference 76">(76)</a> Because the charge storage process utilizes different scan rates in CV experiments, with a varied current response due to different voltage sweeps, CV tests were performed at three scan rates (1, 2.5, and 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>) to study the sodium- and potassium-ion storage mechanisms of MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub> for NIBs and KIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a1 shows CV plots obtained at the zeroth cycle for MoS<sub>2</sub> NIB half-cells at four scan rates (0.5, 1, 2.5, and 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>) to evaluate the interaction of Na<sup>+</sup> with the MoS<sub>2</sub> active material. Clear and distinguishable peaks were observed at 1.11, 1.42, and 2.22 V during the sodiation process and 2.32, 1.58, and 1.38 V during the desodiation process at a scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the zeroth cycle. These peaks were analogous to peaks from the differential capacity curves from the second cycle onward. Although the sodiation peaks move to lower voltages, at higher scan rates of 2.5 and 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the desodiation peaks also moved to higher voltages. Also, at a lower scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the peaks shift to higher or lower voltages during the sodiation and desodiation processes, respectively.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a1) CV, (a2) capacitive vs diffusive contribution, (a3) fitting of anodic and cathodic voltammetric sweep data for MoS<sub>2</sub> NIB; (b1) CV, (b2) capacitive vs diffusive contribution, (b3) fitting of anodic and cathodic voltammetric sweep data for WS<sub>2</sub> NIB; (c1) CV, (c2) capacitive vs diffusive contribution, and (c3) fitting of anodic and cathodic voltammetric sweep data for MoWS<sub>2</sub> NIB.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The CV plots at four scan rates are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b1 for the WS<sub>2</sub> NIB cells. The CV plot at 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b1 demonstrates pronounced peaks at 0.6, 1.25, and 1.9 V during sodiation and 0.4, 0.7, 0.9, and 1.9 V during the desodiation process. At higher scan rates, the sodiation peaks shifted to lower voltages, and the desodiation peaks moved to higher voltages, and at a lower scan rate, the peaks shifted in the opposite direction.</div><div class="NLM_p">The CV plots of the MoWS<sub>2</sub> electrode in the NIB half-cell system are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>c1. Similar to the differential capacity curves, the 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> curve before cycling demonstrated MoS<sub>2</sub> peaks at 2.2, 1.3, and 1.1 V and WS2 peaks at 0.8 V during sodiation. During desodiation for the same scan rate, MoS<sub>2</sub> showed peaks at 2.3 and 1.3 V and WS<sub>2</sub> showed peaks at 0.4 and 1.9 V before cycling. These peaks shifted at lower and higher scan rates of 0.5, 2.5, and 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Similarly, CV plots in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>a1 show peaks for MoS<sub>2</sub> KIB at 1.98, 1.05, and 0.78 V during potassiation and 0.4, 0.78, 1.46, and 1.7 V during the depotassiation process at a scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> at the zeroth cycle. Like NIBs, the potassiation peaks shifted to lower voltages, while the depotassiation peaks moved to higher voltages at higher scan rates in the CV plots obtained before cycling. At a slower scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the intensity of the peaks is also low. The new peaks in the CV plots could be due to a slow scan rate, which allowed K<sup>+</sup>-ions more time to participate in different reactions.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a1) CV, (a2) capacitive vs diffusive contribution, and (a3) fitting of anodic and cathodic voltammetric sweep data for MoS<sub>2</sub> KIB; (b1) CV, (b2) capacitive vs diffusive contribution, and (b3) fitting of anodic and cathodic voltammetric sweep data for WS<sub>2</sub> KIB; and (c1) CV, (c2) capacitive vs diffusive contribution, and (c3) fitting of anodic and cathodic voltammetric sweep data for MoWS<sub>2</sub> KIB.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">CV plots for WS<sub>2</sub> KIB electrodes are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>b1 at four scan rates (0.5, 1, 2.5, and 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>). In the CV plot before cycling, peaks were visible at 1.1 and 1.4 V during depotassiation and only one broad feature was visible at 0.78 V during potassiation at 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Nevertheless, all of the peaks shifted to lower/higher voltages during potassiation/depotassiation at higher scan rates of 2.5 and 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, and shifted to the opposite direction at a lower scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>c1 shows CV plots at various scan rates for MoWS<sub>2</sub> KIB electrodes before cycling. At a slower scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, MoS<sub>2</sub>-like 1.0 V and WS<sub>2</sub>-like 0.7 V peaks were present during the potassiation process before cycling the cell, while the depotassiation process contained MoS<sub>2</sub>-like peaks at 0.4 and 0.9 V and WS<sub>2</sub>-like peaks at 1.2 and 1.6 V. Similar to other electrodes, these peaks shifted slightly at higher and lower scan rates.</div><div class="NLM_p">CV plots were further analyzed to estimate the proportion of surface capacitive effects and diffusion-controlled insertion process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref77 ref78" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref77 ref78" aria-label="reference 77 and 78">(77,78)</a> <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="Equation 3">Equation 3</a> translates the total sum of the contributions<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-45"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-46"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-47"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-48"><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-49" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-50"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-51">i</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-52"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-53" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">(</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-54">V</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-55" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">)</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-56"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-57" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">ν</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-58" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-59">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-60">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-61">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-62"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-63" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-64"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-65"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-66" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">k</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-67" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">1</span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-68"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-69" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">ν</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-70" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-71">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-72">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-73">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-74" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-75"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-76" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">k</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-77" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">2</span></span></span></span></span></span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-3"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mi>i</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>V</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><msup><mi>ν</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><msup><mi>ν</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>2</mn></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>ν<sup>1/2</sup> is the contribution from surface capacitive effects and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> corresponds to the diffusion-controlled intercalation mechanism. From the CV plots, the proportion of contribution was calculated for the zeroth cycle for six cells. The capacity contributions for MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub> electrodes in NIB and KIB systems are illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 7</a>a2–c2 and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a2–c2. As shown, the capacitive contribution increased with an increase in scan rate. The diffusive contributions for the MoS<sub>2</sub> NIB cell were 20, 15, 10, and 7% in the zeroth cycle at scan rates of 0.5, 1, 2.5, and 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, while the diffusive contributions for the MoS<sub>2</sub> KIB cell were 40, 32, 23, and 17% in the zeroth cycle at the same scan rates. Similarly, the diffusive contributions were 37, 30, 21, and 16% for the WS<sub>2</sub> NIB cell and 50, 41, 31, and 24% for the WS<sub>2</sub> KIB cell in the zeroth cycle at the scan rates of 0.5, 1, 2.5, and 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Correspondingly, for the MoWS<sub>2</sub> NIB cells, the diffusive contributions were 38, 30, 21, and 16% in the zeroth cycle, and for the MoWS<sub>2</sub> KIB cells, the diffusive contributions were 55, 46, 35, and 28% in the zeroth cycle at the scan rates of 0.5, 1, 2.5, and 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. According to the capacity contribution plots, MoWS<sub>2</sub> shows the highest diffusive behavior compared to MoS<sub>2</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub> for both NIBs and KIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 7</a>a3–c3 and<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a3–c3 show the fitting of anodic and cathodic voltammetric sweep data for the electrodes for scan rates at 0.5, 1, 2.5, and 7.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. This linear behavior, which is also related to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq 3</a>, allows the calculation of constant values <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>.</div><div class="NLM_p">This study analyzed electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data to investigate the kinetic behavior of as-obtained TMDs and TMD alloy electrodes. EIS measurements were obtained after discharging the cells at the 11th cycle, after ensuring complete relaxation of the half-cells and reaching the open-circuit voltage of 0.1 V.</div><div class="NLM_p">The Nyquist plots of all of the NIB and KIB half-cells showed an inductive loop in the high-frequency stage, which was attributed to the measurement system (wiring to the half-cell) or the cell geometry and windings. The high-frequency intercept of the real impedance axis common to all of the cells corresponded to the sum of internal Ohmic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ohm</sub>), which arises from the electrolyte, active material, current collectors, and electrical contacts. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a> shows a typical semicircular curve in the high-frequency zone in the Nyquist plots of all of the NIB and KIB cells, and the low-frequency zone consists of a diagonal straight line. The area of the semicircles indicates impedance caused by the charge transfer between the electrolyte and the electrode interface. The contact impedance between the electrode interface and electrolyte decreased with a smaller radius. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref79 ref80" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref79 ref80" aria-label="reference 79 and 80">(79,80)</a> Because the SEI formation could not be differentiated from the charge transfer, it was fitted with CPE<sub>dl</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> elements in parallel. The low-frequency stage of the Nyquist plots showed a sloping line or long tail fitted with a Warburg element to reflect the solid-state diffusion. The fitted <i>W–P</i> values always deviated from an ideal condition, indicating the existence of other processes besides solid-state diffusion, such as migration and liquid/electrolyte phase diffusion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a></div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0009.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. Nyquist plot at the 11th cycle for MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub> electrodes in (a) Na-ion half-cells and (b) K-ion half-cells (electrode area = 1.6 cm<sup>2</sup>).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Nyquist plots in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>a for NIBs and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>b for KIBs were fitted with an equivalent circuit using Zview software (version 3.2b, Copyright Scribner Associates, Inc., Southern Pines, NC). The obtained fitted data are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/suppl_file/ao4c01966_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S2 and S3</a>, and the equivalent circuit is illustrated in inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>a. The tabulated values of charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>) revealed that MoS<sub>2</sub> cells have the lowest resistance (10.08 for NIB and 967.5 for KIB). Although the WS<sub>2</sub> cells exhibited the highest charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> = 149.9 for NIB and 2211 for KIB), the <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> is much higher for KIBs than NIBs (i.e., <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> for MoWS<sub>2</sub> NIB = 51.38; <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> for MoWS<sub>2</sub> KIB = 1001).</div><div class="NLM_p">To determine the change in potential over time, this study utilized the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>) by charging/discharging at a 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> current pulse for 15 min, followed by 12 h of relaxation between pulses. During the relaxation period, the magnitude of voltage increased in the insertion process and decreased in the extraction process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref81" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref81" aria-label="reference 81">(81)</a> The diffusion coefficient for Na<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> was determined using the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="equation 4">equation 4</a> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-78"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-79"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-80"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-81"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-82"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-83" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-84" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-85">N</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-86"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-87" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">a</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-88" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-89"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-90" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-91"><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-92" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-93">4</span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-94"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-95">π</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-96">τ</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-97"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-98" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-99" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">(</span></span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-100" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-101"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-102"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-103" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">n</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-104" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">m</span></span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-105"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-106" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">V</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-107" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">m</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-108">S</span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-109" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">)</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-110" style="vertical-align: 1.54em;">2</span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-111"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-112" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-113" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">(</span></span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-114" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-115"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-116">Δ</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-117"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-118" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">E</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-119" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">s</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-120"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-121">Δ</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-122"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-123" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">E</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-124" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">τ</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-125" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">)</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-126" style="vertical-align: 1.54em;">2</span></span></span></span></span></span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-4-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-4"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mn>4</mn><mrow><mi>π</mi><mi>τ</mi></mrow></mfrac><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mi>n</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></msub><msub><mi>V</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></msub></mrow><mi>S</mi></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mi>E</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi></msub></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mi>E</mi><mi>τ</mi></msub></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div>where τ is the duration of the current pulse, which is 15 min or 900 s; <i>n</i><sub>m</sub> is the mole number (for MoS<sub>2</sub> NIB: 4.46 × 10<sup>–6</sup> mol and for KIB: 5.31 × 10<sup>–6</sup> mol); <i>V</i><sub>m</sub> is the molar volume (for MoS<sub>2</sub>: 31.65 cm<sup>3</sup> mol<sup>–1</sup>); <i>S</i> is the total contacting area between the electrode and electrolyte (1.6 cm<sup>2</sup>); Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> is the voltage change between two adjacent equilibrium states; and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> is the voltage change induced by the galvanostatic charge/discharge, shown in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>c. This study showed diffusion coefficient plots for only the MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes, as they showed higher capacity and a consistent trend compared to others.</div><figure id="fig10" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0010.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. Charge and discharge cycles with 15 min of current pulse at a 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> electrode followed by 12 h of relaxation for (a) MoS<sub>2</sub> NIB, (b) WS<sub>2</sub> NIB, (c) MoWS<sub>2</sub> NIB, (d) MoS<sub>2</sub> KIB, (e) WS<sub>2</sub> KIB, and (f) MoWS<sub>2</sub> KIB.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a>a represents the sodium diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>), and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a>b represents the potassium diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>K</sub>) as a function of voltage during charging and discharging for the MoS<sub>2</sub> half-cell. The MoS<sub>2</sub> NIB half-cell exhibited a <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> of ≈10<sup>–16</sup>–10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> during charge and ≈10<sup>–18</sup>–10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> during discharge, which is correlated with previous studies. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref82 ref83" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref82 ref83" aria-label="reference 82 and 83">(82,83)</a> Furthermore, <i>D</i><sub>K</sub> of ≈10<sup>–15</sup>–10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> during charge and ≈10<sup>–14</sup>–10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> during discharge were observed for the MoS<sub>2</sub> KIB half-cells, which is identical to previous works. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref84 ref85 ref86" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref84 ref85 ref86" aria-label="reference 84 to 86">(84−86)</a> Additionally, during the charging state, the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> for WS<sub>2</sub> and MoWS<sub>2</sub> were ≈10<sup>–14</sup>–10<sup>–11</sup> and ≈10<sup>–14</sup>–10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, and during the discharging state, the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> for both were ≈10<sup>–15</sup>–10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>. On the other hand, during the charging state, the <i>D</i><sub>K</sub> for WS<sub>2</sub> and MoWS<sub>2</sub> were ≈10<sup>–14</sup>–10<sup>–13</sup> and ≈10<sup>–15</sup>–10<sup>–14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, and during the discharging state, the <i>D</i><sub>K</sub> for WS<sub>2</sub> were ≈10<sup>–15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and <i>D</i><sub>K</sub> for MoWS<sub>2</sub> was ≈10<sup>–15</sup>–10<sup>–14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>.</div><figure id="fig11" data-index="11" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 11</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0011.jpeg" id="gr11" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 11. Diffusion coefficients as a function of discharge and charge for MoS<sub>2</sub>: (a) NIB and (b) KIB half-cells.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=fig11"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The SEM images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>a1,a2-f1,f2 show a uniform distribution of TMD and TMD alloy nanosheets when mixed with a conductive agent and binder before cycling in the electrode format. The digital camera images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>a3–f3) were visually inspected to compare the conditions of the neat electrodes with electrodes after prolonged cycling. No imperfection or discoloration of the electrodes was visible. However, some electrodes peeled off during disassembly (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>b3 for WS<sub>2</sub> NIB and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>d3 for MoS<sub>2</sub> KIB). After further inspection via SEM, the white substances on top of the electrode seemed to originate from the attached fiber of the separator (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>a5,c5,e5).</div><figure id="fig12" data-index="12" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 12</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/medium/ao4c01966_0012.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0012.jpeg" id="gr12" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 12. (a1, a2–f1, f2) SEM micrographs of pristine electrodes indicating structural integrity before cycling, (a3–f3) digital camera image of the cycled electrodes, (a4, a5–f4, f5) SEM micrographs of cycled electrodes, and (a6–f6) XRF spectra of the electrodes to identify the elemental composition.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/asset/images/large/ao4c01966_0012.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c01966&amp;id=fig12"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>a4,a5–c4,c5 shows that the geometrical characteristics and uniform dispersion of TMD particles were not present for NIBs after the long cycling process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>a1,a2–c1,c2). However, some TMD particles with smooth geometrical shapes remain visible in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>d4,d5-f4,f5 for the KIB cells. The smaller Na-ions may have intercalated into almost all of the TMD particles, but the larger K-ions could only introduce themselves to the surface of the large TMD flakes. Consequently, almost all of the TMD flakes in the NIB cells were broken into irregular smaller particles, while the large TMD flakes in the KIB cells were in mint condition after cycling.</div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>a4–f4, few cracks were observed in the electrodes at low magnifications, while surface cracks in the NIB and KIB electrodes (MoS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, and MoWS<sub>2</sub>) were visible at high magnification (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>a5–f5). However, the surface cracks could be a result of drying the dissembled electrodes on the hot plate at 80 °C for 24 h, resulting in a severe change in volume.</div><div class="NLM_p last">X-ray fluorescence (XRF) of the cycled electrodes demonstrated qualitative elemental analysis, with peaks for Mo, W, and S representing elemental existence after cycling (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>a6–f6). Due to the electrolyte, Na, Cl, and O peaks were observed in all of the NIB half-cells, and K, P, and F peaks were observed in all of the KIB half-cells. Peaks of Cu were detected due to electrode preparation on Cu foil. The elemental compositions of all of the Na-ion and K-ion cells collected from XRF spectra are listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c01966/suppl_file/ao4c01966_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the electrochemical cycling of  Na-ion and K-ions in TMDs (MoS<sub>2</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub>) and TMD alloy (MoWS<sub>2</sub> and MoWSe<sub>2</sub>) nanoflakes was studied and compared for possible use as high capacity electrodes in NIB and KIBs. At the potential range of 0.01–2.5 V, the MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode   demonstrated the highest Na<sup>+</sup> charge capacity (1056 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and the best first cycle Coulombic efficiency (71%). The MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode  also showed the highest K<sup>+</sup> charge capacity (549 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and the best retention capacity (46%) among the KIB half-cells. The fitted cyclic stability plot indicated that Na-ion and K-ion half-cells with MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes possess the highest half-life (NIB: 6.87; KIB: 21.43) compared to other TMD electrodes. The size of the individual constituent atoms  (Mo, W, S, Se) plays a critical role in the alkali-metal ion intercalation process in TMDs. With the increase in  size of the metal-ion, specific capacity and Coulombic efficiency decreased correspondingly.. Based on postcycling analysis almost all of the  large flakes of TMDs have participated in the Na<sup>+</sup> reaction process and nearly all of the large flakes disintergrated into smaller particles. However, for KIBs, the large TMD flakes were obtained almost in pristine condition after even a long cycling process, which indicated the lack of K<sup>+</sup> intercalation and conversion reactions in larger TMD flakes.  Also, the CV and capacitive contribution plots imply that combining tungsten and molybdenum (as in alloy TMD) shows increased diffusive contribution compared to the neat TMDs (the highest diffusive contribution observed at 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>). It is likely that the superior performance of MoS<sub>2</sub> compared to other TMDs and TMD alloys is due to the mismatched crystal structure or large elemental size of the tungsten atom. The electrochemical impedance study further demonstrated that the TMDs with only molybdenum and sulfur (MoS<sub>2</sub>) have the lowest electrical resistance between the electrolyte and electrode. In contrast, the resistance for the TMD alloy with both tungsten and molybdenum (MoWS<sub>2</sub>) or  tungsten (WS<sub>2</sub>) was comparatively higher. This study established the foundation for future investigation of TMD and TMD alloybased electrodes in emerging electrochemical energy storage devices.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div         </div><div class="casAbstract">The current lithium-ion battery (LIB) electrode fabrication process relies heavily on the wet coating process, which uses the environmentally harmful and toxic N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) solvent.  In addn. to being unsustainable, the use of this expensive org. solvent substantially increases the cost of battery prodn., as it needs to be dried and recycled throughout the manufg. process.  Herein, we report an industrially viable and sustainable dry press-coating process that uses the combination of multiwalled carbon nanotubes (MWNTs) and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) as a dry powder composite and etched Al foil as a current collector.  Notably, the mech. strength and performance of the fabricated LiNi0.7Co0.1Mn0.2O2 (NCM712) dry press-coated electrodes (DPCEs) far exceed those of conventional slurry-coated electrodes (SCEs) and give rise to high loading (100 mg cm-2, 17.6 mAh cm-2) with impressive specific energy and volumetric energy d. of 360 Wh kg-1 and 701 Wh L-1, resp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/s41565-020-0657-x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, D.                 10.1038/s41565-020-0657-x</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D15%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D170-180%26journal%3DNat.%2BNanotechnol.%26issue%3D3%26author%3DD.%2BH.%2BS.%2BTan%26author%3DA.%2BBanerjee%26author%3DZ.%2BChen%26author%3DY.%2BS.%2BMeng%26title%3DFrom%2Bnanoscale%2Binterface%2Bcharacterization%2Bto%2Bsustainable%2Benergy%2Bstorage%2Busing%2Ball-solid-state%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fs41565-020-0657-x&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.4c01966&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fs41565-020-0657-x&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">From nanoscale interface characterization to sustainable energy storage using all-solid-state batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tan, Darren H. S.; Banerjee, Abhik; Chen, Zheng; Meng, Ying Shirley</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">170-180</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The recent discovery of highly conductive solid-state electrolytes (SSEs) has led to tremendous progress in the development of all-solid-state batteries (ASSBs).  Though promising, they still face barriers that limit their practical application, such as poor interfacial stability, scalability challenges and prodn. safety.  Addnl., efforts to develop sustainable manufg. of lithium ion batteries are still lacking, with no prevailing strategy developed yet to handle recyclability of ASSBs.  To date, most SSE research has been largely focused on the discovery of novel electrolytes.  Recent review articles have extensively examd. a broad spectrum of these SSEs using evaluation factors such as cond. and chem. stability.  Recognizing this, in this Review we seek to evaluate SSEs beyond conventional factors and offer a perspective on various bulk, interface and nanoscale phenomena that require urgent attention within the scientific community.  We provide a realistic assessment of the current state-of-the-art characterization techniques and evaluate future full cell ASSB prototyping strategies.  We hope to offer rational solns. to overcome some major fundamental obstacles faced by the ASSB community, as well as potential strategies toward a sustainable ASSB recycling model.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1126/sciadv.aav7412"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Approaching high-performance potassium-ion batteries via advanced design strategies and engineering</span>. <i>Sci.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Challenges and future perspectives on sodium and potassium ion batteries for grid-scale energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Wenchao; Lu, Jun; Guo, Zaiping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Today (Oxford, United Kingdom)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The energy crisis and environmental pollution require the advancement of large-scale energy storage techniques.  Among the various commercialized technologies, batteries have attracted enormous attention due to their relatively high energy d. and long cycle life.  Nevertheless, the limited supply and uneven distribution of lithium minerals, as well as their high cost, has greatly hindered the application of lithium-ion batteries in large-scale energy storage.  Therefore, building next-generation alternative rechargeable batteries that feature low cost, long service life, and high safety is of the utmost importance.  Over the past several years, we have witnessed many successful research outcomes for sodium and potassium ion batteries (SIBs and PIBs) in regard to electrode and electrolyte materials.  In this Perspective, we summarize the current developments on SIBs/PIBs and their challenges when facing practical applications, including their cost, energy d., ion diffusivity in solids/electrolytes/interphases, cycle life, and safety concerns.  Furthermore, we provide an overview of strategies to overcome the remaining challenges for achieving better electrochem. performance.  Finally, we outline several possible directions for the future development of these two battery chemistries, with the hope of aiding the transition from the lab. to next-generation practical applications for SIBs/PIBs batteries in grid-scale energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1038/35104644"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J. At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Potassium has similar chem. characteristics compared with lithium while it is more abundant and of low cost, resulting in widespread research attention on potassium-ion batteries (PIBs).  Developing org. polymer cathodes has garnered extensive attention because of their merits of environmental friendship and structure diversity, while confronted with inferior cycle stability and low rate performance.  In this paper, we utilize the low-cost graphite nanosheets to stabilize polyimide (PI@G) for PIBs.  Addnl., the potassium storage mechanism of PI@G was further evaluated; the highly reversible chem. bonds (C=O) of PI@G are responsible to its long-term stability.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Why is sodium-intercalated graphite unstable?</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Moriwake, Hiroki; Kuwabara, Akihide; Fisher, Craig A. J.; Ikuhara, Yuichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">58</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">36550-36554</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">RSCACL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2046-2069</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries offer an attractive low-cost alternative to Li-ion batteries.  Although graphite is used as the neg. electrode in conventional Li-ion batteries, attempts to use it in Na-ion batteries have been hampered by the inability of Na to form graphite intercalation compds. (GICs) under moderate conditions.  It is generally considered that this is due to the size mismatch between Na and the graphite interlayer spacings, but here we show with detailed first-principles calcns. of Li, Na, K, Rb, and Cs GICs that the major reason is the change in chem. bonding between alkali metal (AM) ion and C atoms. van der Waals correction terms are introduced to better reproduce the layered graphite structure, and calcd. formation energies of GICs AMC6, where AM = Li, Na, K, Rb, and Cs, are found to become less neg. (less stable) as ion size decreases from Cs to Na as a result of weakening ionic bonding, until the formation energy of NaC6 becomes pos.  The much smaller Li ion represents an exception to this trend, as its bonds with C atoms contain a covalent component, resulting in a neg. formation energy.  These subtle differences in staging for the different alkali metals explain why graphite is a good intercalation material for Li and K but not for Na.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.5b06809"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jian, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon Electrodes for K-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors for the 1st time report electrochem. K insertion in graphite in a nonaq. electrolyte, which can exhibit a high reversible capacity of 273 mA h/g.  Ex situ XRD studies confirm that KC36, KC24, and KC8 sequentially form upon potassiation, whereas depotassiation recovers graphite through phase transformations in an opposite sequence.  Graphite shows moderate rate capability and relatively fast capacity fading.  To improve the performance of C K-ion anodes, the authors synthesized a non-graphitic soft C that exhibits cyclability and rate capability much superior to that of graphite.  This work may open up a new paradigm toward rechargeable K-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1002/tcr.201700057"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hosaka, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kumakura, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Towards K-Ion and Na-Ion Batteries as “Beyond Li-Ion</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Li-ion battery commercialized by Sony in 1991 has the highest energy-d. among practical rechargeable batteries and is widely used in electronic devices, elec. vehicles, and stationary energy storage system in the world.  Moreover, the battery market is rapidly growing in the world and further fast-growing is expected.  With expansion of the demand and applications, price of lithium and cobalt resources is increasing.  We are, therefore, motivated to study Na- and K-ion batteries for stationary energy storage system because of much abundant Na and K resources and the wide distribution in the world.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exploring the effect of interlayer distance of expanded graphite for sodium ion storage using first principles calculations</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Rathnayake, R. M. N. M.; Duignan, Timothy T.; Searles, Debra J.; Zhao, X. S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">23</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3063-3070</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PPCPFQ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1463-9076</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Expanded graphite (EG) has been shown to be able to store a significant amt. of sodium ions.  Understanding the alkali metal ion storage in EG is of importance for improving EG electrode performance.  In this work, the effect of interlayer distance of pure EG on sodium ion storage was investigated using the d. functional theory calcn. method.  EG structure models with interlayer distances ranging from 3.4 Å to 10.0 Å were simulated.  It was found that EG can store a fairly large amt. of sodium ions through an intercalation mechanism without any contributions from the co-intercalation mechanism or adsorption mechanism if the interlayer distance is larger than 4.4 Å and smaller than 6.0 Å.  It was also found that an interlayer distance of 6.0 Å gives strong binding energy of sodium ions with EG forming thermodynamically stable sodium-graphite intercalation compd. (Na-GIC).  However, when the interlayer distance becomes larger than 6.0 Å, the binding energy between sodium ions and EG becomes weaker.  Computational results have also shown that the enthalpy of formation of the Na-GIC of EG is energetically more favorable when the interlayer distance is increased.  An optimal d-spacing of EG for sodium ion storage was identified in this work.  These findings provide atomistic insights into sodium ion storage in EG, providing guidelines for the design of graphite-based anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.20517/energymater.2023.79"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Trano, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Versaci, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Castellino, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fontana, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fagiolari, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Francia, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bella, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Exploring nature-behaviour relationship of carbon black materials for potassium-ion battery electrodes</span>. <i>Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2024</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">1</span>), <span class="NLM_elocation-id">400008</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A high-voltage organic framework for high-performance Na- and K-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jiande; Liu, Xuelian; Jia, He; Apostol, Petru; Guo, Xiaolong; Lucaccioni, Fabio; Zhang, Xiaozhe; Zhu, Qi; Morari, Cristian; Gohy, Jean-Francois; Vlad, Alexandru</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">668-674</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The org. metal-ion battery field remains challenged by the lack of high-voltage alkali-cation reservoir cathode materials.  Whereas a few recent breakthroughs provided valuable solns. for Li-ion storage, Na-ion and K-ion org. reservoirs with high voltage and ambient stability remain elusive.  Herein, we show that the versatile benzene-1,2,4,5-tetrayltetrakis methylsulfonyl-amide (PTtSA) tetra-anionic framework displays universal performance for alkali cation storage.  The new synthesized Na4-PTtSA and K4-PTtSA phases reversibly exchange two Na+ or K+ equiv. per formula unit at redox potentials of 2.5 V vs Na+/Na and 2.6 V vs K+/K, resp.  A singular comparative anal. of Li-, Na-, and K-ion phases discloses the impact of the alkali cation on the physicochem. properties, with direct impact on the electrochem. of the materials.  This work not only offers guidance and principles to tune the redox properties of org. redox materials via spectator cations but also highlights the versatility of org. materials for alkali cation storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1038/s41598-024-53154-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tanibata, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nonaka, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Makino, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Takeda, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nakayama, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Chloride electrode composed of ubiquitous elements for high-energy-density all-solid-state sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Sci.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrolyte Design Strategies for Non-Aqueous High-Voltage Potassium-Based Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tan, Hong; Lin, Xiuyi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Molecules</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  High-voltage potassium-based batteries are promising alternatives for lithium-ion batteries as next-generation energy storage devices.  The stability and reversibility of such systems depend largely on the properties of the corresponding electrolytes.  This review first presents major challenges for high-voltage electrolytes, such as electrolyte decompn., parasitic side reactions, and current collector corrosion.  Then, the state-of-the-art modification strategies for traditional ester and ether-based org. electrolytes are scrutinized and discussed, including high concn., localized high concn./weakly solvating strategy, multi-ion strategy, and addn. of high-voltage additives.  Besides, research advances of other promising electrolyte systems, such as potassium-based ionic liqs. and solid-state-electrolytes are also summarized.  Finally, prospective future research directions are proposed to further enhance the oxidative stability and non-corrosiveness of electrolytes for high-voltage potassium batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.202200294"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Trano, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Corsini, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pascuzzi, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Giove, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fagiolari, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amici, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Francia, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Turri, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bodoardo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Griffini, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bella, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lignin as Polymer Electrolyte Precursor for Stable and Sustainable Potassium Batteries</span>. <i>ChemSusChem</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>15</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">12</span>), <span class="NLM_elocation-id">e202200294</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lignin as Polymer Electrolyte Precursor for Stable and Sustainable Potassium Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Trano, Sabrina; Corsini, Francesca; Pascuzzi, Giuseppe; Giove, Elisabetta; Fagiolari, Lucia; Amici, Julia; Francia, Carlotta; Turri, Stefano; Bodoardo, Silvia; Griffini, Gianmarco; Bella, Federico</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemSusChem</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Potassium batteries show interesting peculiarities as large-scale energy storage systems and, in this scenario, the formulation of polymer electrolytes obtained from sustainable resources or waste-derived products represents a milestone activity.  In this study, a lignin-based membrane is designed by crosslinking a pre-oxidized Kraft lignin matrix with an ethoxylated difunctional oligomer, leading to self-standing membranes that are able to incorporate solvated potassium salts.  The in-depth electrochem. characterization highlights a wide stability window (up to 4 V) and an ionic cond. exceeding 10-3 S cm-1 at ambient temp.  When potassium metal cell prototypes are assembled, the lignin-based electrolyte attains significant c. performances, with an initial specific capacity of 168 mAh g-1 at 0.05 A g-1 and an excellent operation for more than 200 cycles, which is an unprecedented outcome for biosourced systems in potassium batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1016/j.susmat.2022.e00504"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gandolfo, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amici, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fagiolari, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Francia, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bodoardo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bella, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Designing photocured macromolecular matrices for stable potassium batteries</span>. <i>Sustainable Mater.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Adding salt to expand voltage window of humid ionic liquids</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Ming; Wu, Jiedu; Ye, Ting; Ye, Jinyu; Zhao, Chang; Bi, Sheng; Yan, Jiawei; Mao, Bingwei; Feng, Guang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Humid hydrophobic ionic liqs.-widely used as electrolytes-have narrowed electrochem. windows due to the involvement of water, absorbed on the electrode surface, in electrolysis.  In this work, we performed mol. dynamics simulations to explore effects of adding Li salt in humid ionic liqs. on the water adsorbed on the electrode surface.  Results reveal that most of the water mols. are pushed away from both cathode and anode, by adding salt.  The water remaining on the electrode is almost bound with Li+, having significantly lowered activity.  The Li+-bonding and re-arrangement of the surface-adsorbed water both facilitate the inhibition of water electrolysis, and thus prevent the redn. of electrochem. windows of humid hydrophobic ionic liqs.  This finding is testified by cyclic voltammetry measurements where salt-in-humid ionic liqs. exhibit enlarged electrochem. windows.  Our work provides the underlying mechanism and a simple but practical approach for protection of humid ionic liqs. from electrochem. performance degrdn.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1073/pnas.2314408120"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qi, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Chemical and spatial dual-confinement engineering for stable Na-S batteries with approximately 100% capacity retention</span>. <i>Proc.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Transition metal dichalcogenides for alkali metal ion batteries: engineering strategies at the atomic level</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Biao; Chao, Dongliang; Liu, Enzuo; Jaroniec, Mietek; Zhao, Naiqin; Qiao, Shi-Zhang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1096-1131</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  In the past few decades, great effort has been made toward the prepn. and development of advanced transition metal dichalcogenide (TMD) materials for anodes of alkali metal ion batteries (AMIBs).  However, their electrochem. performance is still severely impaired by structural aggregation and fracture during the conversion reaction.  To address these issues, various methodologies for the fabrication of hierarchical and hybrid nanostructures, with optimization of materials and electrodes, have been fully investigated and reviewed.  As regards tuning the TMD-based materials, extensive efforts have been undertaken toward optimization of their intrinsic structure at the at. level, including surface defects, interlayer spacing expansion, phase control, alloying, and heteroatom doping.  However, the design strategies and methods to manipulate the intrinsic structures and electrochem. mechanisms in AMIBs have not been fully summarized.  This review provides a well-timed and crit. appraisal of recent advances in the engineering of TMDs at the at. level for AMIBs, by combining computational and exptl. approaches.  The correlation between these strategies and electrochem. performance is highlighted.  The challenges and opportunities in this research field are also outlined.  We expect that this review would be beneficial for improving the overall knowledge on the charge storage mechanisms in TMDs and for pointing out the importance of intrinsic structure engineering for enhancing the performance of TMDs in energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref27" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501937"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cook, J. This study shows that metal dichalcogenides are promising potassium anode materials for emerging K-ion, K-O2, and K-S batteries.
